Open-Ended Investment Companies (Amendment) Regulations 2005

Lord McIntosh of Haringey rose to move, That the draft regulations laid before the House on 10 February be approved [11th Report from the Joint Committee].

The noble Lord said: My Lords, I shall also speak to the draft Financial Services and Markets Act 2000 (Carrying on Regulated Activities By Way of Business) (Amendment) Order 2005.

In November 2003, the then Financial Secretary to the Treasury announced the main strands of the two-year review of the Financial Services and Markets Act 2000—which hereinafter will be called FSMA—in a Statement to Parliament. The review was carried out in light of the Government's commitment to review the impact of FSMA on competition in financial services. This commitment was made in response to the March 2000 Cruickshank report on competition in UK banking. In addition, the Government took this opportunity to review certain components of the FSMA framework more generally, including the scope of FSMA in places.

The review involved three main strands: first, an assessment by the Office of Fair Trading of the impact of FSMA on competition in financial services; secondly, changes to aspects of the Financial Services Authority's practices, and possible changes to the Financial Ombudsman Service; thirdly, changes in the boundary of regulation.

In December 2004, the Financial Secretary to the Treasury announced the outcome of the review in a statement to Parliament. The outcome included plans by the Government to adopt a deregulatory package of reforms to improve the functioning of financial regulation. These would simplify certain regulatory requirements, redraw the boundary of regulation in a range of places where appropriate, and enable the FSA to operate more efficiently and effectively. The draft statutory instruments before us today will implement some of the reforms of this package.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 336
 

The draft Open-Ended Investment Companies (Amendment) Regulations 2005 have twice been put out for public consultation: once in February 2004, and then in January 2005. The draft regulations have received widespread support from the parties who expressed a view on them. The regulations will provide open-ended investment companies—or OEICs—with a range of freedoms to conduct their business more effectively and to compete on a more even basis with authorised unit trusts, which I shall not call "AUTs". These regulations will achieve this by removing certain costly regulatory requirements which provide limited investor protection, if any.

For example, OEICs are currently required to hold annual general meetings, or AGMs. OEIC business usually reserved for AGMs covers matters such as presenting the OEIC's accounts and the reports of the directors and the auditors on the accounts; appointing or reappointing the OEIC's auditors, and viewing or re-electing the OEIC's directors with the exception of the OEIC's authorised corporate director, or ACD. Directors' service contracts can also be inspected at AGMs.

However, AGMs are costly to organise. Holding an AGM costs between £5,000 and £10,000. Yet shareholders have shown little interest or inclination to attend them. Furthermore, some of the business which may be conducted by AGMs is not actually undertaken in practice, such as electing and re-electing directors. Consultation respondents explained that, in reality, almost all OEICs do not have any other directors apart from their authorised corporate director, whose appointment and conduct is not subject to consideration at AGMs.

Other AGM business can be transacted in more cost-effective ways, without harming investor protection. For example, even though OEIC accounts are currently laid before an AGM, they are already issued to each OEIC shareholder separately. This makes the AGM business largely unnecessary. Directors' service contracts are currently available for inspection at AGMs, but these could be made available to shareholders at their request throughout the year instead.

Dispensing with AGMs for OEICs should bring the OEIC regime more in line with that of authorised unit trusts. This will allow OEICs to compete on a fairer basis, since authorised unit trusts are not required to hold AGMs. The authorised unit trust's equivalent of an authorised corporate director appoints and reappoints its auditors. Similarly, these draft regulations propose that OEIC auditors and directors may be appointed and reappointed by their ACD.

The regulations will also allow the FSA to regulate OEICs more efficiently, by easing certain requirements placed on the FSA. Currently, when the FSA decides to approve an OEIC proposal after having previously issued a warning notice proposing to refuse it, the FSA is required to issue a decision notice.

However, decision notices are subject to more requirements than written notices under the FSMA. Consultation respondents felt that those additional
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 337
 
requirements are not appropriate in the circumstances. For example, one of the additional requirements relates to the recipient's right to have his or her matter referred to the Financial Services and Markets Tribunal. Yet it is not clear why someone who has had their proposal approved would require that. The regulations therefore remove the requirement for the FSA to issue a decision notice in these circumstances and enable it to issue a written notice instead, for which the requirements are somewhat less onerous.

Currently, the FSA must publish in the London or Edinburgh Gazettes notifications that the FSA has issued or received certain OEIC documents. That includes documents evidencing alterations in the OEIC's instrument of incorporation and documents setting out any changes in the address of the company's head office. If one of the reasons for publication in the Gazettes is to ensure that shareholders are notified of changes to the OEIC, other existing requirements in the OEIC regulations and in the FSA's rules should achieve that already. Publication in the Gazettes adds nothing.

In summary, the regulations should lead to the regulation of OEICs becoming less onerous, without putting at risk investor protection, and to the FSMA framework continuing to facilitate competition.

The second statutory instrument, the Financial Services and Markets Act 2000 (Carrying on Regulated Activities by Way of Business) (Amendment) Order 2005, has also been put out twice to public consultation in February 2004 and again in January 2005, and has received broad support from the parties who expressed views.

The Myners review examined the factors distorting institutional investment decisions. That argued that existing regulation was one of those factors. In particular, in his November 2000 interim report, Paul Myners claimed that regulation at the time discriminated against investment by occupational pension fund trustees in relation to particular types of product. This discrimination in particular concerned private equity vis-à-vis quoted equities. The Government accepted that argument and made secondary legislation under FSMA to address it. However some concerns have remained that FSMA continues to discriminate in this way. The Treasury has also received several requests further to deregulate the investment activities carried out by pension fund trustees.

The second statutory instrument addresses those concerns and the problems that stem from them—in particular, problems that the current legislative arrangements for occupational pensions trustees are causing trustees to take too narrow a range of investment decisions; invest in too narrow a range of products; and take too narrow a range of investment advice.

Let me explain the current situation for unauthorised occupational pensions fund trustees who want to invest on behalf of a fund. Such trustees need to be authorised by the FSA, unless they delegate all routine or day-to-day decisions to an authorised fund manager. The order will refine the definition of decisions that unauthorised occupational pension fund trustees are not permitted to take. That should enable trustees to make a wider range of investment decisions.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 338
 

At present, it is thought that the combination of "routine" and "day to day" imposes tougher regulation on trustees than under the predecessor legislation that existed before FSMA. That referred only to day-to-day decisions. For example, choosing which annuity to buy is, according to the FSA, generally not a day-to-day decision, but could be regarded as a routine decision. That could lead trustees to make too narrow a range of investment decisions. The order therefore amends "routine or day to day decisions" to "day to day decisions" only.

In addition, the Pensions Act 1995 allows trustees to make their own investment decisions, subject to certain conditions. Although trustees are not currently subject to formal legal requirements showing that they possess the knowledge and understanding to carry out their trustee duties, from April 2006, the Pensions Act 2004 will legally require trustees to hold more knowledge about investments. Allowing trustees under FSMA to make a wider range of investment decisions in the way provided for in the order will therefore be consistent with the Pensions Act 1995, while working in tandem with the changes which flow from the Pensions Act 2004.

This order will also enable unauthorised occupational pension fund trustees to make a wider range of investments, especially in pooled investment vehicles and in contracts of insurance. Those products are useful investment options for many pension schemes. They generally involve similar or lower risks compared with investments in private equity limited partnerships, in which trustees can already invest. Moreover, allowing trustees to invest in those additional products, which carry similar or lower risks than the products in which trustees can already invest, is in line with the Government's and FSA's approach to favouring risk-based regulation.

The order also enables trustees to "obtain and consider" advice rather than requiring them to "act in accordance" with it. Allowing trustees to invest only in accordance with advice may limit the ability of trustees to invest in a wider range of products, because many advisors tend to focus on particular products only.

Since trustees are subject to a general duty of care, and are unlikely to ignore expert advice without good reason, I am confident that this change should not harm investor protection. If trustees are enabled to challenge advice, they may engage more in investment decisions and the quality of advice provided to them should improve.

Finally, the order enables trustees to take advice from a wider range of sources, particularly from professional firms which operate under Part 20 of FSMA. These include actuarial, legal and accountancy firms.

Part 20 of FSMA allows professionals to carry on certain regulated activities, which include providing advice to pension fund trustees, without being FSA-authorised.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 339
 

Since Part 20 of FSMA ensures that these professionals are supervised and regulated by their professional bodies, and are subject to the conditions set by Part 20 of FSMA, investor protection should not be harmed by allowing these firms to provide advice.

This change should have the added benefit of increasing competition among investment advisors, thereby improving the cost and quality of advice provided to occupational pension fund trustees. This should contribute to improved investment performance.

In conclusion, in order to ensure that the regulation of OEICs becomes more proportionate, that the FSMA framework continues to facilitate competition, and that the performance of occupational pension funds is enhanced, I commend the first of these regulations and this order to the House. I beg to move.

Moved, That the draft regulations laid before the House on 10 February be approved [11th Report from the Joint Committee].—(Lord McIntosh of Haringey.)

Social Security (Contributions) (Re-rating and National Insurance Funds Payments) Order 2005

Lord McIntosh of Haringey rose to move, That the draft order laid before the House on 1 February be approved [8th Report from the Joint Committee].

The noble Lord said: My Lords, I am pleased to introduce this order which deals with various national insurance contribution rates and thresholds. The provisions in the order are compatible with the European Convention on Human Rights. All the changes were announced at the time of the Pre-Budget Report.

For the self-employed, it raises the small earnings exception below which, depending on their level of profits, they may claim exemption from paying Class 2 contributions. The exception will rise in April from £4,215 to £4,345 a year—an increase broadly in line with prices. Many people choose to pay these contributions in order to protect their benefit entitlement. The rate of Class 2 contributions for 2005-06 will rise to £2.10 a week, also in line with prices.

Staying with the self-employed, the draft order also sets the profit limits between which main rate Class 4 contributions are paid, the lower limit at which contributions become due, and that the upper limit will increase broadly in line with inflation. The lower limit will rise in line with the income tax personal allowance from £4,745 to £4,895 a year. At the upper end of the scale, the upper profits limit will continue to match the upper earnings limit for employees, which will rise to £32,760 for 2005-06. This ensures that the self-employed pay main rate Class 4 contributions on much the same range of earnings as employees liable for Class 1 contributions and is an essential element in making the national insurance system fair for everyone.

The draft order also deals with the weekly rate of voluntary Class 3 contributions which help those with insufficient contribution records in any given tax year
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 343
 
to make up a qualifying year for benefit purposes. The rate of Class 3 contributions will rise in April by 20p to £7.35 per week, a standard re-rating in line with prices.

The review of contribution rates is accompanied by a report from the Government Actuary detailing the effect of the draft order and the draft order uprating benefits laid by the Secretary of State for Work and Pensions on the National Insurance Fund. I am pleased to say that for the sixth year in a row, there is no expectation that the fund will need a Treasury grant. Nevertheless, a prudent provision of 2 per cent of all benefit expenditure is made.

Northern Ireland has a separate national insurance scheme from Great Britain, but the two schemes are closely co-ordinated and maintain parity of contribution rates. This draft order covers both Great Britain and Northern Ireland. I beg to move.

The child trust fund scheme will begin in April and will be administered by the Inland Revenue. Child trust fund appeals will follow the temporary arrangements for tax credits and will be heard by appeal tribunals which hear social security appeals. There will be a further right of appeal to the social security commissioners. These arrangements will continue until the planned reform of the tax commissioners. The regulations will cease to have effect when an order is made under Section 24(1) of the Child Trust Funds Act 2004.

The first draft statutory instrument makes regulations for procedures for appeal tribunals in Great Britain. The second mirrors these arrangements for Northern Ireland and comes before this House because child trust funds are an excepted matter under the Northern Ireland Act. The final draft instrument provides for the procedures for the social security commissioners.

The first and third sets of regulations are made under powers in the Social Security Act 1998, and the second set under the corresponding Social Security (Northern Ireland) Order 1998. The Act and order have been applied and modified by the Child Trust Funds (Non-tax Appeals) Regulations 2005, made on 3 February. Together they make provision for administering and deciding child trust fund appeals.

Regulation 5 prescribes that only the personal representative of an individual, apart from the board, who has died may proceed with an appeal. This regulation does not apply where the appellant is an account provider as this will be a financial institution. Regulation 6 sets out the composition of an appeal tribunal hearing child trust fund cases. Most cases will be heard by a single legally qualified panel member. Cases raising complex financial issues may be heard by a legal and a financial panel member.

Regulations 7 to 11 and 13 to 25 deal with procedures and broadly mirror the provisions of the main Social Security and Child Support (Decisions and Appeals) Regulations 1999.

Regulation 12 is similar to Regulation 44 of the main appeal regulations. It goes a little wider to provide for the whereabouts of the child or relevant person to be kept confidential where requested and, interestingly, includes a child for whom a local authority has parental responsibility.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 346
 

The Social Security Commissioners (Procedure) (Child Trust Funds) Regulations 2005 largely mirror the existing Social Security Commissioners (Procedure) Regulations 1999. The main differences in this set of regulations relate to the omission of benefit-related references which are not applicable to child trust funds and the procedure to be followed in dealing with penalty appeals. Appeals involving penalties are not subject to the requirement to obtain leave to appeal to the commissioners.

Regulation 13(1) prescribes the time limit for giving notice of appeal to the commissioners on a penalty. This is within one month of the decision of the appeal tribunal being sent to the applicant. In all other cases, it is within one month of the appellant being sent written notice that leave to appeal has been granted.

Regulations 20 and 21 relate to procedures concerning oral hearings. Regulation 20(2) requires a commissioner to hold an oral hearing where a party appealing against the imposition of a penalty requests one. Regulation 21(6) prescribes the list of those who are entitled to attend the hearing and be heard. In addition, where the case involves a penalty appeal, Regulation 21(9)(a) provides that the leave of the commissioner is not required where the person on whom the penalty has been imposed wishes to give evidence, call or question witnesses.

I am sorry, but I did some work on this—I suspect this might of interest to the noble Lord, Lord Oakeshott—and the countries among the G8 with the highest savings ratios at the moment, running between 10 and 11 per cent, are Germany and Italy, and they have the lowest wealth and income per head of around $40,000, as indicated by GDP. The UK has a savings ratio of around 5.6 per cent and a wealth income of around $46,000 to $48,000 per head. GDP per individual is the only one we have. The US has a savings ratio of 1.5 per cent and a wealth per head of $55,000.

That shows that the savings ratio is no reliable indicator of the wealth per head of the country. More, given that the savings ratio does not include housing wealth, and that it is simply the difference between the denominator and the numerator of pensions—the difference between savings paid in for pensions and pensions taken out—it does not indicate anything except rainy-day contingency funds. I am sure I have told the noble Baroness nothing she does not already know, and I wish the Benches opposite would get off this savings ratio hook, as if it indicates something profound about what is happening in this country.

Having said that, the Government have done well in redistributing income, in the sense of bringing the poorest up. When we came into office, a child on
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column 350
 
income support would have had from the current Government an income through income support of just under £17 a week. Had that been RPI'd in today's terms, it would be worth about £23 a week. It is actually now more than £50 a week and has more than doubled in real terms. So we have made a significant contribution to attacking child poverty as reflected in income. However, there are real problems in the distribution of wealth and this, though only a modest gesture, goes some way towards meeting those problems.

More than a quarter, or 28 per cent, of UK households, have no savings at all. Of households earning less than £300 a week, more than 40 per cent have no savings at all. If to that £250, or £500 if the child is on an income-related benefit, the parents were able to put away the child benefit for that child—it is probably not likely in most cases, but let us imagine that they could—that child at 18 would have a fund, whether in cash or equities, depending on the assumptions you make of growth, of something between £20,000 and £25,000. That is a substantial sum. And that is without the addition of possible additions at seven and, perhaps, possible additions at secondary age.

In the same way as we are trying to ensure that the poorest children have seen a real increase in their income, so we are trying to ensure that the poorer children also go into adulthood with a modest capital endowment from the Government on behalf of us all. If the parents and any other members of the family are able to contribute, there will be a more substantial one, which will free those children to make the sort of choices—to go to university and so on—which children from more affluent households can more or less take for granted.

Finally, the noble Lord, Lord Oakeshott, made a point with which I am entirely sympathetic, which is to use national savings as a vehicle. I believe that that vehicle has great trust in the country, and I should like it to come in a variety of forms—in pensions for women, through to the child trust fund. I shall try to find out whether we expect national savings to be a player. But one possibility for stakeholder funds, with their 1.5 per cent cap, that may go some way to meeting a concern that I share, is that there may be a degree of lifestyling, if I can use that phrase. When the child is 14 or 15 years old, you could move the money out of equities into a safer bond, gilt or cash-linked account, so that there is no possibility of a plunge in its value before the child is able to draw down that money at 18 for university, college or whatever else seems appropriate.

I hope that I have answered the questions and that I have had a swipe at the false play on the savings ratio figures. I do not doubt that if I have not persuaded the noble Baroness, Lady Noakes, on that point, we shall revisit it in future.

On Question, Motion agreed to.

New Opportunities Fund (Specification of Initiative) Order 2005

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Culture, Media and Sport (Lord McIntosh of Haringey) rose to move, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the New Opportunities Fund (Specification of Initiative) Order 2005.

The noble Lord said: The order will enable the Big Lottery Fund, as the New Opportunities Fund and the Community Fund are now known, to make transformational grants. I shall explain what is meant by transformational grants. They will fund big projects to transform communities, regions or the nation as a whole, similar to some of those funded by the Millennium Commission. Indeed, the roots of the order lie in the Millennium Commission, and had we decided to keep the Millennium Commission running, this statutory instrument would not have been necessary. However, the Millennium Commission ceased to receive any new lottery money in August 2001.

The commission leaves behind a successful legacy of vision and innovation and funding major infrastructure schemes—successes such as the Eden Project in Cornwall or the Millennium Stadium in Cardiff or Our Dynamic Earth—the world's first geological visitor centre in Edinburgh. In addition, there have been smaller community projects, such as the one that transformed over 1,500 acres of industrial wasteland into a coastal parkland—the Millennium Coastal Park in Llanelli or Mile End Park, an ecology park with a new arts pavilion and sports facility, in London's East End.

None of us wants to see such good work come to an end. We therefore believe that it is sensible for one of the lottery distributors to carry forward the best of the commission's work. We want the Big Lottery Fund to take on the mantle of the Millennium Commission by occasionally supporting exciting new capital projects with the potential to contribute to growth and transformation across the United Kingdom.

There has already been considerable consultation on the transformational grants programme. The programme was outlined in the National Lottery Funding Decision Document in July 2003. It was also included in both phases of the Big Lottery Fund's own consultation. Responses to those consultations have shown there is overall broad support for the continuation of lottery funding for major capital projects.

The transformational grants programme will fund major capital projects that do not fit into the specific remits of other lottery distributors but will benefit community regeneration that might otherwise not be funded. Developing the transformational grants programme itself is not, of course, for government, but is rather the work of the Big Lottery Fund. Lottery money is not government money, but we set the broad framework in the way for which the legislation provides, and the Big Lottery Fund carries the work forward.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC119
 

It is right to brief the Committee now about the transformational grants programme as it has been developed by the Big Lottery Fund. It is essentially a UK-wide capital grants programme but with a small non-core revenue funding stream to ensure that projects are sustainable and to promote access to capital facilities. The proposed budget is £140 million, of which £20 million will be set aside to ensure adequate provision for development grants.

The remaining £120 million would comprise one very large project—about £50 million—possibly linked to a television programme, that will engage and inspire the public to vote on a range of options, rather like the "Restoration" series on historic buildings at risk, and smaller grants, under a separate strand, of between £10 million and £25 million each. The idea of public voting linked to a television programme is an exciting development. It reflects our determination that in future the public should have much more of a say in how lottery money is spent.

One of the principles of consideration will be regional and country spread. The Big Lottery Fund is committed to consulting country interests, as the programme further develops. The programme is still under development by the Big Lottery Fund, but it estimates that the stage 1 decisions—the shortlist—will be made in about May 2006. The public vote—the stage 2 award—will take place in about May 2007. It is a long-term process and rightly so, but to allow the Big Lottery Fund to progress the programme work we need this statutory instrument now.

The New Opportunities Fund and the Community Fund—now operating jointly under the name of the Big Lottery Fund—applied to the Secretary of State in December 2004 to authorise the two funds to participate in a joint scheme to make transformational grants. The Community Fund already has powers to make transformational grants but the New Opportunities Fund does not. That is because NOF may distribute money only to projects that are designed to give effect to initiatives specified in an order by the Secretary of State under Section 43B of the National Lottery etc. Act 1993. The new initiative in this order adds to the existing four NOF initiatives to allow NOF to fund transformational grants and therefore to participate in the joint scheme.

I must make it clear that the fact that we are bringing forward the order is not directly related to the progress of the National Lottery Bill, which is currently before Parliament awaiting its Second Reading in the House of Commons.

The shadow Big Lottery Fund, which is what the fund should perhaps more properly be called in advance of legislation, made it clear to us back in December that it wanted to move ahead with the transformational grants before the earliest possible date that legislation could be passed. Even if there had been a Second Reading of the Bill in December last year, we would still be debating the order.

The statutory instrument will empower the Big Lottery Fund to make transformational grants within a broad high-level framework. The programme is still
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC120
 
being developed by the fund, but I believe that it will allow the vision, spirit and innovation of the Millennium Commission to continue. It will enable the Big Lottery Fund to fund projects which can not only inspire but also have a real and lasting impact. It will benefit the regeneration of communities throughout the United Kingdom. I commend the order to the Committee. I beg to move.

Moved, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the New Opportunities Fund (Specification of Initiative) Order 2005.—(Lord McIntosh of Haringey.)

Contracting Out (Functions in Relation to Cultural Objects) Order 2005

Lord McIntosh of Haringey rose to move, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the Contracting Out (Functions in Relation to Cultural Objects) Order 2005.

The noble Lord said: The order is being made to implement many of the recommendations in the January 2004 report, Securing the Best for Our Museums: Private Giving and Government Support, to Her Majesty's Treasury by Sir Nicholas Goodison, who was commissioned to look into ways of improving the effectiveness and efficiency of support to regional and national museums, to help them to acquire works of art that might otherwise be sold abroad. I should say that the majority of the recommendations were for the Treasury to implement. The present order addresses the minority of the Goodison recommendations that were for the Department for Culture, Media and Sport, and I hope that the Committee will agree that the department has made very satisfactory progress on implementing them. I have arranged for a consolidated report on DCMS's response to the Goodison recommendations to be deposited in the Libraries of both Houses.

Sir Nicholas's report highlighted the continuing risk to the quality of UK collections of art arising from the loss of many cultural items to overseas buyers. The recommendations in the Goodison report for changes to the administration of certain programmes are intended to address that risk by ensuring that a number of functions currently split between DCMS and the Museums, Libraries and Archives Council will be transferred to MLA, so that a more consistent, customer-focused service can be offered. We expect that to lead to more people using the services.

The statutory functions are: the acceptance of property offered in lieu of inheritance tax under Section 230 of the Inheritance Tax (Formerly Capital Transfer Tax) Act 1984 and Section 12 of the National Heritage Act 1980, together with the allocation of that property and the power to receive or make payments in relation to it under Sections 9 and 10 of that Act; the power to make indemnities under Section 16 of the 1980 Act for loss or damage suffered to items lent to institutions in the United Kingdom and the requirement to make a report on indemnities given
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC123
 
annually to Parliament under Section 16A of that Act; and the power to issue a licence for the export of cultural goods under Articles 2 and 3 of the Export of Objects of Cultural Interest (Control) Order 2003.

The non-statutory function that we are transferring is the provision of the secretariat for the Reviewing Committee on the Export of Works of Art. It advises the Secretary of State on whether a work of art or a cultural object for which an application for a permanent export licence has been received is of national importance and, therefore, on whether export of the object should be deferred and offers invited to acquire the item for the nation. That transfer does not require legislation, but is part of the package of administrative changes that the order will enable.

The order paves the way for the transfer of those functions from DCMS to MLA. Sir Nicholas pointed out that those were all executive functions that sat rather uneasily in DCMS, which was otherwise a strategic body. He envisaged that the transfer of the functions to MLA would help to,

    "reduce confusion, improve knowledge and provide an impartial and free source of authoritative advice and guidance".

We endorse that view.

Once the order has been passed, we will enter into an agreement with MLA authorising it to exercise the functions on behalf of the Secretary of State and provide related services to the department. MLA will be responsible for the administration of all the procedures for acceptance of property and objects in lieu of inheritance tax and their allocation for display to the public. As I have already said, that responsibility is currently split between DCMS and MLA. The acceptance in lieu scheme is very well administered by MLA at present, and we have full confidence that it will continue to be so.

We have, however, decided that there should be an exception: the acceptance of items to be displayed to the public in situ on the estate from which they were offered. The display of such items by the original owners is governed by an agreement between the estate and the museum that takes possession after allocation. Contrary to the Goodison proposals, we believe that it is more appropriate that, for now, final approval of offers in situ should remain a matter for Ministers. That decision has been taken in light of the high value of the items that tend to be offered under such arrangements and the public interest in ensuring continuing access to such items.

The government indemnity scheme facilitates public access to items of an artistic, historic, scientific or technological nature. The scheme covers loaned items made accessible to the public in a temporary exhibition or on long-term loan, made available to the public and for study. The scheme underwrites the borrower's risk of loss of or damage to the objects while on loan to the borrower or while in transit to and from the institution. That is how most of the major exhibitions in the United Kingdom can be held. Current examples of institutions that have benefited in such a way are the "Turks" exhibition at the Royal Academy and the recently
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC124
 
opened "Arts and Crafts" exhibition at the Victoria and Albert Museum. MLA currently administers the government indemnity scheme for non-national museums. We intend to give it responsibility for national museums and institutions.

The Government, however, incur substantial contingent liability under the government indemnity scheme—£3 billion to £4 billion at any one time. Changes to the terms of the indemnity offered could significantly increase that liability. We therefore feel it important to ensure that, in the rare instances where changes are requested to the terms of the indemnity, the Secretary of State's and the taxpayers' interests are protected by ensuring that final approval on any changes to the terms of the indemnities will remain with the Secretary of State.

MLA will be authorised to issue export licences for objects of cultural interest, in line with the Goodison recommendations. MLA will also take on responsibility for providing the secretariat to the Reviewing Committee on the Export of Works of Art. That does not require legislation, but responsibility will transfer with the functions delegated on the authority of the order. It makes particularly good sense for those functions to be transferred together, as they form part of the same system of export control.

We have decided not to accept Sir Nicholas's recommendation that MLA should also take on responsibility for taking the final decision to defer an export licence. That occurs where the reviewing committee considers that an object is of national importance and its export should be temporarily halted, pending offers to acquire the object for the nation. Sir Nicholas saw that as the sort of executive task that could be undertaken by MLA; we feel that it is crucial that Ministers should continue to take such decisions. The prevention of export on the recommendation of the reviewing committee amounts to interference by the Secretary of State in people's property rights on the basis of the public interest and must be justifiable and fully defensible. The procedure restricts the applicant's right to the peaceful enjoyment of his possessions, a right that is protected under the Human Rights Act 1998. That right makes it all the more important that the decision continue to be taken by the Secretary of State, who is answerable to Parliament for that decision and is best placed to assess the public interest.

The intention is for MLA to take on responsibility for acceptance in lieu of inheritance tax, the government indemnity scheme and the Reviewing Committee on the Export of Works of Art from 1 April 2005—Friday of next week. MLA will assume responsibility for the issuing of export licences a little later, in May 2005. I assure the Committee that the proposals enjoy widespread support. MLA was consulted extensively by the Department for Culture, Media and Sport during the preparation of the statutory instrument, and it was fully involved in the discussions about the terms of the agreement, under which functions will be delegated. Sir Nicholas Goodison's review consulted widely. Finally, I am satisfied that the draft order before the Committee is compatible with the European Convention on Human Rights.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC125
 

The public are entitled to expect high standards in the four areas that we propose to delegate to MLA. I am confident that the move will provide a sound basis for building on the excellent service already provided to the public. I assure the Committee of DCMS's and MLA's ongoing commitment to the successful execution of the functions. I beg to move.

Moved, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the Contracting Out (Functions in Relation to Cultural Objects) Order 2005.—(Lord McIntosh of Haringey.)

Lord McIntosh of Haringey: I am grateful to both noble Lords for welcoming the order, and I am happy to answer the questions arising.

23 Mar 2005 : Column GC126
 
announced that annual funding for the National Heritage Memorial Fund would double by 2007–08, compared to 2004–05. In the medium to longer term, we think that the transfer will produce benefits in terms of efficiency gains and a better service to the users of the functions which are being transferred.

In staffing terms, about eight posts are being transferred from DCMS to MLA. We are fortunate that five members of staff have agreed to transfer to MLA. That will ensure continuity of expertise within the transferred functions.

Bovine Tuberculosis

The Countess of Mar rose to ask Her Majesty's Government whether they will reconsider their policy on bovine tuberculosis in light of concerns expressed by more than 350 veterinary surgeons to the Secretary of State for Environment, Food and Rural Affairs about the spread of the disease.

The noble Countess said: The Secretary of State, Margaret Beckett, in her introduction to the publication, Government strategic framework for the sustainable control of bovine tuberculosis (bTB) in Great Britain, states:
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC127
 

    "Bovine TB continues to be one of the most difficult animal health problems that we face. The Government has a role in leading and facilitating the changes required to reverse the long-term upward disease trend, but we can only move forward on a partnership basis. The farming industry must play their part, recognising the benefits to them of reduced disease incidence".

I declare my interest as a farmer, working in partnership with my husband on our small farm in Worcestershire in the middle of a TB hotspot. Nearly two years ago my husband saw which way the wind was blowing and sold all but two young heifer calves from our pedigree Blonde d'Aquitaine herd. We also have goats and sheep. I understand that both species are vulnerable to bovine TB, perhaps goats more so than sheep. The figures for these animals are not known because they are not routinely tested.

Now rarely a week goes by when we do not hear that one or another of our dairy or beef farming neighbours has had a herd breakdown. Many of these farmers and the private and government veterinary surgeons, who have to deal with the aftermath of an outbreak of bTB in a herd, feel somewhat insulted by the last sentence in my quotation from Mrs Beckett. They are all desperate to be allowed to play their part in reducing, if not eliminating, the incidence of this very damaging disease. Government policies prevent them doing so. Talking shops and inappropriate scientific research do nothing to halt the relentless march of the bacterium through our countryside.

A few months ago some Defra officials travelling in a train from Worcester to London were overheard discussing what they had discovered at the Worcester offices. They, too, seemed to be in despair. They felt that the disease was out of control and were suggesting that some way must be found to tell English farmers that they would have to face an England with no cattle. If that is the opinion that pertains within Defra, what hope have our farmers?

The Government's strategy for bTB is peppered with visions. I have a picture of the authors squatting in a Delphic cave somewhere smoking pot. Having visions for the future is simply not good enough. The situation is so serious that action is required now. The Government seem to be in thrall to a small number of activists who will not accept that, if the disease is to be controlled, those wild animals that are known to be a reservoir for bTB must also be controlled. It is not good enough to tell farmers that they must take measures to ensure biosecurity when the suggested measures are extremely expensive and, particularly where badgers are concerned, virtually useless.

In our own case, the winter before last we spent £5,000 having the fences and gates to all our permanent pasture fields renewed. In one field, the badgers had, within weeks, built a sett over the top of the fence. This allowed our sheep to stray. In another field, they have totally undermined the fence and the field is slipping down a bank. How can we stop badgers drinking from water troughs which have to be low enough for sheep to reach? It is unreasonable to expect us to put a 240-volt electric fence of several strands around the fields, particularly as we have public footpaths across those fields.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC128
 

Can we take just a few minutes to assess the cost to the taxpayer and to the farming community of preserving the badger? The Minister has been heard to say that if a cost-effective and humane way of preventing bTB can be found, the Government will look at it seriously. On a very rough calculation based on figures that the Minister gave me in a written reply yesterday, if we say that the badger population has increased to 500,000 since the last estimates were made in the mid-1990s and, in the light of that increase, we take the higher figure for the annual mean cost of damage to agriculture, noting that that does not include the cost of badger damage to our waterways—£500,00 for the Llangollen Canal this year, for example—and the 300 badger setts on the railway lines in the south-western region, with compensation and other direct costs, that works out at about £2,250 a badger. That is somewhat more than the price of an average cow.

Bovine tuberculosis was first found in badgers in 1971. There has been a proven association between badger populations and the incidence of bTB in cattle from two major trials that took place in the Thornbury area near Bristol between 1975 and 1981, East Offaly in 1988 and, now, the Irish four counties trial, which was reported at the beginning of this year. All those reports came to the conclusion that the badger is a significant reservoir of infection.

In my county of Worcestershire, where it borders on Herefordshire and Shropshire, some 80 per cent of the new herd breakdowns have been attributed by Defra vets to wildlife. That generally means badgers. Most of the herds are closed and have been for some years. There is no nose-to-nose contact with cattle from other herds and no shared equipment between farms; arable fields bound the farms so that grazing land is generally in the centre; and there is no direct or indirect human contact. These farms are getting homebred reactors out of the blue. What they all have in common is significant evidence of badgers. The remaining 20 per cent of breakdowns can be attributed to cattle movement but, even here, I suggest that the infection may well be secondary. Beef breeders sell on their young stock for fattening. Dairymen sell young heifers as milking replacements. It could well be that the cattle that are sold are already infected by badgers at the farm of origin but are not yet responding to the skin test because the load of the infection is insufficient to provoke the animals' immune response.

Thus it is easy for Defra and Professor Bourne to attribute the breakdown to animal movements when, in fact, it is due to wildlife infection at the farm of origin. Cattle-to-cattle infections are also considered to be secondary. The primary infection may be in one or two animals that have been grazing through the summer. When cattle are housed in the autumn, they are in close contact with one another and, lo and behold, by the following spring there is a major breakdown.

I would be one of the first to admit that badgers are beautiful creatures and that they enhance our countryside. Every farmer to whom I have spoken likes to see them about, but they also say, as if with one voice, "as long as they are healthy". Since the Badgers Act 1973—a measure to prevent badger baiting—their
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC129
 
numbers have increased exponentially, especially in the west of England and the Midlands, where the digging is easy. Badgers have no natural predators except the motor car, and it is obvious that, as a population of any creature rises, there is likely to be an increase in stress, which will mean that they fight for territory and food, and that leads to increased susceptibility to injury and disease.

Once acquired, bTB spreads within the badger population both from wounds that turn septic and infect liver and kidneys, and from airborne infection from the lungs of infected animals. A badger sett provides ideal conditions for the bacterium to multiply. Badgers are omnivores. They travel fields and woodland in search of nourishment. They break into buildings with their strong claws to raid food stores and chicken coops. They urinate and defecate as they go. It has been found that 1 ml of infected badger urine can contain 300,000 bTB bacteria, which is enough to infect a bullock. How can farmers possibly protect their land and buildings from such opportunist creatures?

We hear little of the suffering of infected badgers. It seems that if there are more than eight badgers in an infected group the disease becomes endemic. Many social groups have 30 or 40 badgers in them. In Hereford and Worcester between 1996 and 1998, more than a third of badger carcasses examined by MAFF were infected. In some badger removals after farm breakdowns, more than 50 per cent of the animals were infected. As farmers, we are required by law to ensure that the animals in our charge are healthy. It can be said that we are required to look after the badgers on our farms, for we are not allowed to frighten them away or dispose of them by more radical means. Is not Defra being a bit two-faced when it enforces the law for one group of creatures while allowing another group to suffer?

Much is being made of the fact that we must wait for the results of the Krebs trials before any conclusions can be drawn about badger involvement. There are serious concerns about the robustness of these trials. Already we have seen that one part of the trial has been stopped because it was believed to be causing an increase in herd reactors. It may be that the wrong things were being measured. In the Puddlestone area, the trial started in December 2002. Badgers were killed between August 2003 and November 2003. Is it possible that cattle were infected between December 2002 and August 2003? Has that also happened in other areas?

There is a school which believes that the proactive cull is also ineffective because the method used is inefficient: the frequency of badger removal is at too low a level and not enough badgers are being removed. This can be compared with the Irish trial where snares were used. In it, 20 days were taken to clear setts and clearances were conducted three times a year for a week or 10 days. The Krebs trials involve cages, many of which have been sabotaged, and once yearly clearances at irregular times and a low level. Previously, cyanide gas was used to clear setts. This was stopped following experiments at Porton Down that showed that the animals suffered a prolonged and painful death. I understand that those experiments
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC130
 
were flawed and the cyanide works very much more effectively in a sett where it is moist. If the snares used by the Irish are deemed unacceptable in England, would the Minister listen to those who know about the way in which cyanide gas works? I also ask him why Defra consistently ignores the advice of Professor Charles Godfray, who has repeatedly questioned the integrity of the Krebs trials?

Defra seems to be sitting in the middle of the road like a rabbit blinded by headlights. While it is doing that, it does not seem to appreciate the disaster that is occurring in the darkness. Farmers are shell-shocked when a breakdown occurs within their herd. They cannot sell or move their cattle except to slaughter. The value of pedigree animals tumbles, as breeders are forbidden from attending shows, their shop window. Their land loses value because it harbours infection. They are rendered impotent when they know that only part of the solution is in their own hands.

The animals that they lose to slaughter are not, as one noble Lord suggested during debates on FMD, merely units of production. They are frequently the result of many years of careful breeding by grandfather, father and son. The animals are as much part of the family as their dog or cat. Each animal has its own identification and is known to those who handle it. I do not think that the public quite understand the relationship a farmer and his family have with their cows.

Similarly, veterinary surgeons have a relationship with their clients. Vets are trained to save lives wherever possible. It is soul-destroying for them, too, to have to condemn an animal to death, as those who undertake TB testing day in and day out have to do. This is why that letter was written to the Secretary of State. I asked a practising vet last week what he thought should be done. He said:

    "I think all of us vets would say we need to deal with the source of infection, which is accepted as being badgers in 60–80 per cent of cases. I believe the test is good and should not be criticised too heavily and recrudescence is likely to be a minor part of the problem. TB seems to be the only disease with which I have ever dealt in which I am not allowed to deal with the source.

    "My concern, and I think that of the 300 vets, is that we don't have time to wait for the results of the trial, and there are obvious flaws in its design. Those trial areas which do not receive any treatment, ie reactive and survey only, may need to give farmers additional funding to put up with having no controls so as to keep their support for the trial".

He goes on to suggest that outside the trial, some form of badger culling has to be undertaken, either by Defra operatives alone or by landowners under Defra wildlife unit control. He is sure that, if that is done effectively, the results should dramatically reduce the number of breakdowns within a couple of years.

If my plea falls on deaf ears, perhaps the Minister will consider the alternative of legalising badger farming. I understand that smoked badger hams taste delicious, and there could be an excellent trade in shaving brushes and sporrans. The added bonus is that the badger population would be a healthy one.

4.15 p.m.

Lord Soulsby of Swaffham Prior: We should thank the noble Countess, Lady Mar, for setting down this important question on livestock in this country.

Some 50 years ago, in the 1940s and early 1950s, many veterinary surgeons in the United Kingdom—including myself—were engaged in tuberculin testing of cattle as part of a national programme to eradicate tuberculosis from the national herd. It was a boring task, often demanding long hours in inclement weather and the danger of being trampled on by cattle. Occasionally, one injected oneself inadvertently. However, we did it in the belief that we were helping to eradicate a major scourge of cattle—bovine tuberculosis, which causes their painful and protracted death.

Another major concern at that time was the transmission of the infection to man via infected milk, until pasteurisation of milk removed that danger. However, as some people may well know, individuals who contracted tuberculosis as children may have reactivated lesions as they grow older and their immune system is less than it should be. Nevertheless, there was sustained national effort of tuberculin testing, the creation of the "attested herds" scheme, the selling of TT-tested milk at a premium, segregation of cattle at cattle markets, restraints on movement, pasteurisation of milk and improved meat inspection. This scourge of cattle and humans was controlled, and many—including myself—hoped that it was eradicated. Apart from pockets of infection in the south-west of the United Kingdom, it very nearly was.

That was the case then. Unfortunately, as we heard from the noble Countess, it is not the case now. Since 1971—when bovine TB was discovered in badgers—TB in cattle and badgers has progressively increased, such that up to 30 per cent of badgers in hot-spot areas previously free are now infected. There is a steep rise in the incidence of TB reactors in cattle, too. It is projected that TB outbreaks in cattle will be around 3,000 in 2004 when the sums are added up, compared with 88 in 1986.

The relationship of TB in badgers to TB in cattle has generated much debate, especially on the part of conservationists. It is certain that the TB organism in cattle and badgers is the same. It circulates in the badger and cattle populations, and has entered into wildlife such as deer and other species. The crucial question is whether infected badgers are the reservoirs for the infection in cattle and have a role in the introduction of TB into cattle in clean areas. The evidence is very strong that they do.

The noble Countess referred to the recent studies in the Republic of Ireland—the "four areas" trial—where the culling of badgers was undertaken. They provide,

    "by far the clearest experimental demonstration to date that badgers play a role in bovine TB in cattle",

as commented by Professor Godfray, an independent consultant. The Independent Scientific Group on Cattle TB, chaired by Professor John Bourne, agrees that the Irish study provides strong evidence that badgers have a role in propagating TB in cattle.
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC132
 
Professor Dalton, the chief scientific adviser to Defra, says that it is the best evidence yet that the badger contributes to TB in cattle. Our own trials, as referred to by the noble Countess, provide comparable evidence following badger clearances in south Avon, Devon and Dorset in this country.

With that evidence behind us, what are the next steps? A call has been made by 350 veterinarians for more strategic culling of badgers to control the spread of tuberculosis in cattle. They call for a halt to what at present is an inexorable rise in, and spread of, infection over the past two decades and the failure of TB control policies to control the disease.

I believe that enough information is probably available from other strategic culling exercises to develop a more effective culling programme, taking note of the fact that, in its latest report, the Independent Scientific Group on Cattle TB identified the lack of a coherent and dependable science basis for TB control policy as a severe constraint on progress.

The way forward has been set out by Defra in a 10-year programme for the control of TB. There are 12 strategic goals, including increased surveillance, studies of disease transmission, development of improved diagnostic techniques—especially the use and development of the gamma interferon test—and the development of an effective vaccine for cattle and badgers.

Let us hope that those proposed strategic frameworks will encompass the strategic culling of badgers, as called for by the many veterinarians who recently signed the letter to the Secretary of State. Perhaps I may point out that many have had first-hand experience of bovine tuberculosis. They hope that the control of movement described in the document will go forward without delay.

We have controlled bovine TB in the past—we very nearly eradicated it. The issue is now more difficult with the badger playing an important role in the transmission of, and serving as a reservoir for, the disease. Nevertheless, a co-ordinated approach involving all stakeholders makes eradication an achievable goal, which we very nearly reached in the past. However, it requires a sustained effort by all.

slaughtered. I attribute that massive outbreak to autumn grazing by the cows on a kale field just below a steep bank that is infested with badgers. I now anxiously await the results of the next test, which are due this month.

My neighbours in east Somerset, which is a prime dairying area, have also suffered serious losses because of tuberculosis. The honourable Member for Frome and Somerton has already drawn attention in another place to the damage to long-established dairy herds in that TB hotspot.

I suggest that the first thing to be done is to remove for ever the sentimental idea that the badger is a nice, cuddly, little animal, not far removed from Easter bunnies and sweet little foxes. Though badgers feed largely on worms, grubs and beetles, they destroy the nests of ground-nesting birds and eat the eggs if they can. As has been mentioned, badgers have no natural predators to control their numbers. As a result, numbers have increased substantially since badgers received full protection. Badgers are savage and dangerous if cornered and are quite capable of biting off fingers or thumbs or inflicting serious wounds. The badger's only enemy today is the motor vehicle. Badgers have, no doubt, caused road accidents and consequent injuries. They are also capable of infecting water supplies, as I know from experience.

We must be clear about the amount of damage caused by bovine TB. The number of animals slaughtered in the years up to and including 2001 was less than 10,000 a year. In the three years since then, the number of cases where stock was killed because of TB has shot up to over 22,000 a year and peaked—perhaps, as we do not yet know—in 2003 at 23,168. The compensation paid by the Government in 2002–03 was nearly £32 million and rose in the following year. It is forecast to be £36 million for the current year, 2004–05. That makes a total of £102 million in three years, with very little prospect of reduction.

Farmers suffer further consequential losses and extra expenses because of standstill orders. They cannot insure against such losses unless they were covered before the epidemic began.

I urge the Government to remove the total protection now enjoyed by badgers because so many of their carcases are found to be severely infected with TB. I imagine that badger urine and faeces—and perhaps badger breath—are highly infectious. In my case, badgers march regularly through my best grazing pastures and there is almost nothing that I can do to stop them. Why should badgers be more highly protected than other mammals?

The Government should stop the endless prevarication and should pay attention to what vets are now telling them. They should act on the evidence published in the scientific press about the experiments in four non-contiguous counties of the Republic of Ireland, which were referred to earlier. Those experiments showed that badger culling produced large reductions in TB infections in cattle.

got those animals; we have had so many calves born this week". Every bull calf is knocked on the head, because there is no way that they can handle the animals that are being born. My main point is that the Government have to be seen to do something now to address the wildlife side of the situation. So what can be done? The most obvious approach would seem to be the use of BCG as a vaccine for badgers. BCG is an old vaccine that was used in the past to counteract TB in humans, but it has proven to be very effective in controlling or dampening down bovine TB in badgers in Ireland and, more especially, in possums in New Zealand.

There is a debate about whether to use oral bait, which might have implications outside the badger population as opposed to the more difficult but safer subcutaneous procedure, but I shall not go into that here. I gather that a new ELISA blood test can be used in the fields, which takes 10 minutes to give a result. It is approximately 80 per cent sensitive in detecting BTB in badgers. If Defra is continuing its policy of trapping, a good way forward would surely be to use these tests on trapped badgers. If the sett has BTB, the badgers should be culled in their entirety; if not, they should be vaccinated.

I stress again my main point that the Government have to be seen to be doing something now to alleviate this wave of bovine TB that is rolling over us. Society has stopped farmers, or anyone else for that matter, controlling the badger population. As a result, the situation is now completely out of hand, particularly in the south-west. It seems only fair and right that, if the Government have removed the option of self-help, they must do something to redress the balance.

Of course, the Government were right to attempt to resolve the TB problems through research. I feel certain that when the Krebs trials began, everyone felt a sense of hope—indeed, some degree of aspiration—that these problems could be overcome. But the truth is that virtually all those at the coal-face—by that I mean the farmers and the vets—now believe that the trials have lost all credibility and are leading nowhere.

There are a number of reasons for this lack of confidence in the Krebs trials. The first is because of the profound disruption that occurred due to the foot and mouth outbreak. That was no one's fault. The second is because so many trapped badgers were released by animal rights protestors. That did not help the situation. Thirdly, there was the cessation of the reactive culling element of the trial. I am not certain why that ceased but I think that it was highly regrettable, particularly as it was so conclusive in the Irish experiment.

Another fact of which I was made aware only yesterday—the noble Countess, Lady Mar, referred to it and I was interested in what she said—is that the Krebs trial used box traps, to which the badger soon got wise, whereas in Ireland they used snares. Another factor in the Krebs trial not being as effective as it might have been was that some 10 to 20 per cent of farmers within the culling areas refused to co-operate at all. So the net result from the trial is that we are firmly in no man's land, without any clear way forward.

23 Mar 2005 : Column GC139
 

That closed herds are now being diagnosed with TB on an equal basis with others is, in itself, a sure sign that matters have got worse, despite the fact that field experience shows that cattle-to-cattle transmission is relatively insignificant. However, I am aware that there is some disagreement on that point between the local vets and the Independent Scientific Group on Cattle TB.

Mr Denny, the Worcestershire vet who has written to a number of your Lordships, maintains that badgers infected with TB can take up to three or four years to die. He also legitimately asks why those who wish to preserve badgers at all costs are not more concerned about the welfare consequences of the badgers. That never seems to be raised at all, and it is a fundamental point.

Mr Denny goes on to point out that during the latter stages of infection, when the badgers become very ill, they are expelled from their setts and invade farms, which of course provide an easy source of food. Consequently, the opportunity to spread TB through their saliva and urine to housed cattle brings a further dimension to the problem and adds further evidence on why closed herds are now equally susceptible to outbreaks of TB.

All those to whom I have spoken who deal with the problem on a regular basis now feel that TB is more or less out of control. Waiting for the results of the Krebs trial is no longer an option. As a vet told me, it has,

    "been invalidated by so many factors that it is unlikely to give a meaningful result".

Everyone agrees that there is a reservoir of infection within the wildlife population, emanating mainly from badgers, which almost certainly came from cattle in the first place but is now being fed back to them.

The problem therefore needs to be addressed from both sides. When a reservoir of infection on a farm is identified, that must be effectively dealt with at the same time at which the infected cattle are culled. I am bound to ask at this juncture why a badger is perceived by some as more valuable than a dairy cow. If the dual approach is not undertaken with commitment and vigour, what is the point of continuing to cull thousands of cattle at considerable cost to the taxpayer and providing vets and farmers alike with what I can describe only as a false dawn?

What is required above all else is for the current level of uncertainty to be lifted. A clear lead given by government should be combined with a genuinely honest look at the facts. In the absence of a vaccine—it is some way away—there are only two clear options. First, it is for the Government to say that agriculture will simply have to live with TB and that farmers should adapt their management accordingly. That would negate the need for testing, culling—except in extreme cases—and, of course, compensation. Although that may be seen as a perfectly reasonable option by some, the industry would feel very badly let down if the Government took that view. I am told that there would be a danger that, whereas TB is not now in itself a major animal health issue, it may well become so. Furthermore, I have heard
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC140
 
it suggested that it could evolve to such an aggressive state that eventually it might contaminate milk, despite pasteurisation.

The second option is of course to control the disease effectively, which is after all an EU directive. That is presumably what the Government would like to do. The public would like that as well and, from my discussions, I believe that it is what vets and farmers would like above all else. It is sometimes difficult to know exactly what the animal welfare lobby would like—short-term culls to eliminate the problem or long-term suffering for badgers and cattle. That seems fairly easy to answer.

As has been suggested, control has already been achieved in New Zealand where the reservoir was possums, and Australia where it lay with the buffalo. The government in Australia carried out an aggressive cull on the wild buffalo in the areas where bovine TB had existed, and they culled infected stock. It was highly successful. I think that I am right in saying that Australia has now enjoyed TB-free status for some three years.

So it can be done. But if the UK is going to succeed, I suggest that a clear and positive line must be taken by the Government, unpopular though that may be in certain quarters.

cattle. Badgers must be tested as well as cattle. It is a case of equal treatment for cattle and badgers. It is quite simple. Indeed, that ought to be the Defra policy.

United Nations: Rights of the Child

Baroness Walmsley rose to ask Her Majesty's Government what progress has been made on the recommendations of the United Nations Committee on the Rights of the Child Report 2002.

The noble Baroness said: I ask the Question today because of what I believe is the UK's continuing failure to embrace the Convention on the Rights of the Child as the framework for children's policy and service development. The Every Child Matters: Change for Children programme is silent on children's human rights, and there is little sign that the Government are acting on the strong recommendations of the 2002 report of the Committee on the Rights of the Child.

The convention's principles and standards are binding on states that have ratified it. The UK ratified the CRC in 1991 and, in doing so, agreed to implement the full range of economic, social, cultural, civil and political rights of children. State parties must submit
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC149
 
to a report every five years. In September 2002, the Committee on the Rights of the Child carried out an examination of UK progress in meeting its obligations to children. The committee made 78 recommendations for improvements in law, policy and practice. Each year since then, the Children's Rights Alliance for England has carried out a comprehensive analysis of government action on the recommendations. The most recent report last November showed that there had been significant progress on just 17 of the 78.

The first recommendations that I want to highlight relate to the structures by which the Government deal with the issues. To ensure that governments uphold the human rights of children, the committee recommended that the Government assign the co-ordination of the implementation of the convention to a highly visible and easily identifiable permanent body with an adequate mandate and resources. Since then, the Children and Young People's Unit in the Department for Education and Skills has been replaced with the Children, Young People and Families Directorate. Soon after the directorate was established, the international team dealing with the Convention on the Rights of the Child was disbanded. There is now no identifiable body or group of officials with responsibility for the Convention on the Rights of the Child.

The committee praised the Government's plan to develop a national children's strategy and welcomed the promise that it would be underpinned by the convention. However, since then, the Government have abandoned their plan for a national children's strategy and have, instead, published a Change for Children programme for local children's services authorities. None of the Change for Children publications mentions the Convention on the Rights of the Child; and none describes government plans for upholding children's human rights.

The five outcome goals of the Children Act 2004 are no substitute for a comprehensive plan of action on children's human rights. The Minister for Children, Young People and Families has, remarkably, claimed that the outcome goals are more comprehensive than the convention with its 54 articles. Many children's organisations, including the NSPCC, have expressed concern that adequate resources should be given to the local safeguarding children boards, with their new burden to investigate and review child deaths. Can the Minister confirm that that will be the case?

The convention requires that children's best interests should be a primary consideration in all matters affecting them. It gives children a wide range of rights that I do not have time to detail today. The Minister may argue that some of those rights are implicit in the five outcome goals. But the Government are required to use the Convention on the Rights of the Child explicitly and systematically as the benchmark for meeting all the needs of all children. In many policy areas and in our overall approach, we are lagging far behind other countries.

The Government have brought in human rights legislation—I welcome that—and promote it in other countries. For example, the Foreign and Commonwealth
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC150
 
Office recently published its human rights strategy, which reiterates the importance of every government upholding human rights. It says:

    "Human rights represent universal standards that transcend cultural and national boundaries, and that reflect principles and teachings in all the major faiths. They are developed and codified in international law by individual UN treaties, of which six treaties are considered to provide the core of international human rights".

One of those treaties is the UNCRC. It is hypocritical for the Government to expect Commonwealth countries to implement the convention while flouting several of its basic tenets themselves. The cultural transformation that the Government and others seek for children is reliant on respecting and upholding their human rights. Without a clear commitment across government to the systematic implementation of the convention, we shall continue to lack a unifying vision of good childhood that serves all children in all situations.

I now turn to specific areas of policy that give cause for concern. During the passage of what is now the Children Act 2004, we had lengthy debates about the abolition of the defence of reasonable chastisement for physical assaults on children—a matter about which the Government had come under severe criticism by the committee, both in 1995 and 2002. We also debated the objectives of the new Children's Commissioner for England and the UK—the independent champion for children called for by the convention.

I and others begged the Government to base policy in both those areas on their commitments under the convention. We did not succeed, except in amending the reasonable chastisement area of law in a way that left the courts without clear direction, parents confused and children without equal protection under the law governing assault. How do the Government plan to monitor the effect of the Lester amendment? Will they report to Parliament? Although our new commissioner must have regard to the UNCRC, he has the weakest powers of any of the UK commissioners, as well as those across the rest of Europe. No wonder we were disappointed, but we wish him luck and will watch his work with interest.

Two other major policy areas cause great concern. One is juvenile justice, on which there are numerous issues but I shall mention only seven of them. First, the UK has one of the lowest ages of criminal responsibility in the world, at the age of 10. Most European countries set their age of criminal responsibility between 14 and 16. The Committee on the Rights of the Child urged the UK to "increase considerably" the age of criminal responsibility. Nothing has been done.

Secondly, criminal justice programmes now aim to prevent children as young as eight becoming involved in crime. A decade ago, that was the task of the social services and other welfare agencies. The Joint Committee on Human Rights has warned the Government that the criminal justice system is the "wrong tool" to intervene in young children's lives.

Thirdly, the Government's response to the committee agrees that custody should be used only as a last resort and that the incarceration of children in
 
23 Mar 2005 : Column GC151
 
prison should be reduced to an absolute minimum. Yet the UK locks up more children than most other industrialised countries.

Fourthly, there have been 28 child deaths in custody since 1990, but there has never been a public inquiry concerning a child death in custody. Fifthly, control and restraint has been used 11,593 times on children aged between 12 and 14 in secure training centres in the past four years. That is unacceptable.

Sixthly, in March 2004 the Prisons Minister informed Parliament that "inmates" in young offender institutions during 2002-03 got on average only 7.1 hours per week of education. According to the Forum on Prisoner Education, that has been falling over the past four years, for which figures are available. The Chief Inspector of Prisons recently stated that no juvenile institution was meeting the target of 30 hours per week. Children have a right to education under the convention.

Seventhly, 71 children were locked up for breach of an anti-social behaviour order between June 2000 and December 2002. The Government have changed the law so that children subject to ASBOs can be vilified in the local and national press. That is in clear breach of Article 40(2) of the Convention on the Rights of the Child, which grants children the absolute right to privacy at all stages of court proceedings. I could go on, but, instead, I look forward to the Minister telling us how the Government intend to rectify this terrible situation.

The second major area of concern is asylum-seeking children. The detention of children for the purposes of immigration control runs contrary to international standards on the treatment of children and prisoners as set out by the UNCRC and the UNHCR. In recognition of the particular vulnerabilities of children, international law places the needs of children above the requirements of immigration control. Yet the UK Government put immigration law before the rights and welfare of asylum-seeking children through their reservation from the convention. The reservation was criticised in both the 1995 and 2002 reports.

That reservation has been widely interpreted to exclude asylum-seeking children, subject to immigration control, from all the provisions of the UNCRC. This is illustrated most pertinently by Home Office proposals forcibly to return separated children to their countries of origin. The prison officers that I have met when visiting some young offender institutions are mainly very committed to what they are doing. They are enthusiastic in wanting to do the best by the young people in their institutions, even though they have little specific training in working with children. In particular, interpersonal skills are vital to hold children in these settings.
